Miss Yang's Secret 207

— By Wenzhi

By Gu Jianchen

A Three-Act Play

When will the life of Chinese drama become enriched? Judging by the current state, it seems a distant prospect. The general public's acceptance still wallows in clumsy performances and ugly techniques of old plays. No matter how outdated and devoid of artistic meaning these old plays are, their indulgence remains intensely interested. Moreover, drama is suspended in mid-air. How many good performances are presented to us by those who claim to strive in this field? Even in terms of quantity alone, how many people truly stand on the stage? Can we represent them with significant numbers? Since it is already so impoverished in this aspect, how can we even discuss other issues? This might be because drama in China has not received substantial stimulation, leading to its consistent decline into a crisis of stagnation. What thoughts will arise when we see such a fragmented situation?

We can't even discuss the artistic expression of the stage. How much power is generated by the few public performances we see in a year? However, we can't be too critical of this. While scripts may lack perfection in technical aspects due to the absence of stage technology, we must wait for good, new stages before we can have scripts that represent a new spirit and possess perfect techniques. But currently, our situation has not reached that level, and our strength is insufficient to bear this heavy burden. In many ways, it is clear that the development of our drama will see scripts precede the stage. There is already a slight tendency in this direction, and it will continue to be so in the near future. This is naturally because the advancement of scripts is less hindered than that of the stage. I believe that the sign of Chinese drama's flourishing will definitely lie in the emergence of brave and daring scripts that overthrow everything, representing a new spirit. Therefore, our most urgent task is to continue waging a defensive war for drama, promoting, explaining, and resisting to achieve a genuine understanding and acceptance from society, thereby promoting the rebirth of our stage. This surge of momentum is no accident. We need to actively start by using our strength.

However, when will the dawn of this new era begin? I have no obligation to answer this. It depends on the reading of the temperature gauge of our drama's atmosphere, especially the quality and quantity of scripts are prerequisites.

I have inadvertently discussed some issues concerning Chinese drama itself. It is true that creating dramatic art is difficult, but it is not worthwhile to be daunted by this. Now, let's talk about Mr. Gu Jianchen's "Miss Yang's Secret." I certainly don't want to say that this script will achieve any particular effect, but in today's drama scene, it can be considered a relatively good work. Mr. Gu has published two plays in recent years, one is "The Gentry Boss" and the other is "Miss Yang's Secret." "The Gentry Boss" will be introduced later. "Miss Yang's Secret" seems to have been performed before with decent results, so it seems to have received its evaluation on the stage as well.

The story of "Miss Yang's Secret" goes like this:

Zheng Lie, the newly appointed Chief of Public Security, considers his first achievement to be the implementation of a gambling ban. He believes that "it is easy to leave a bad legacy for an administrative officer in a place, but it is difficult to achieve a virtuous policy. Now that I have found such a good opportunity, it is not easy to come by, and I cannot let it go" (page 90). Therefore, he is determined to eliminate the Great China Club, a roulette gambling den. He resists the lobbying of local tyrants and evil gentry like Zhou Jize, vowing that "under my authority, I certainly cannot allow such a harmful and disruptive institution to exist for even one day." However, he does not know who the owner of this Great China Club actually is. He has not yet sorted out his own background. He does not know that the owner of the Great China Club is actually Miss Yang, his nominal sister but in reality his mother. Thus, Miss Yang's secret is revealed in this helpless situation.

Miss Yang understands that the situation is getting worse and worse. Amidst the battle of various confused emotions, she desperately wants to close down the Great China Club. She knows that continuing will only further wrong people, but she hopes for a peaceful solution without losing face. Moreover, she also wants to close down the Great China Club because of Zhou Jize's "relentless entanglement, which I find frightening, so I want to close down the Great China Club to avoid him using his status as a protector to harass me" (page 58). However, she ultimately fails to escape due to environmental factors.

At the same time, she is provoked by Jize, and she decides to deal with Zheng Lie herself, disregarding everything, wanting him to consider the past and not excessively pursue the Great China Club, leaving a way out for people to reform themselves. But as a result, she cannot directly use her own name as the owner, so she has to pretend to sympathize with the female owner of the Great China Club, using subtle words to persuade him. Naturally, she is extremely saddened by the rejection, but she still uses excuses to compare herself, constantly inserting the implication of "if it were me." Later, in uncontrollable tension, she painfully and intermittently reveals Miss Yang's background: "She is from Luoyang, Henan. She is a graduate of a women's normal school... She became a widow at the age of seventeen. Less than thirty-five days after her husband died, she gave birth to her unfortunate child, but her husband's parents had died long ago. Her husband's inheritance harmed her. Unscrupulous relatives bullied her for being weak and coveted her property, forcing her to remarry. She was alone and helpless to deal with them, so she valued her reputation and her child. She thought of various ways to escape to Shanghai, initially wanting to seek help from her classmates, unwilling to be bullied by her relatives again. She was born with a proud temper and wanted to be economically independent to support her child. Of course, the child was also brought to

Page 24

Shanghai... for the sake of her child, she dared to take this risk. Later, she found a teaching job, but how much money could she earn teaching in a primary school in a month? The cost of living in Shanghai was so high, how could she survive? She had no choice but to accept the advice of her classmate, Miss Pan, to organize the Great China Club. She thought about working, but she couldn't do it. She knew that the available paths could not support the lives of both mother and child, so she had to follow Miss Pan to do that inhumane work. In short, she was barely surviving. She had no good way to die." Zheng Lie only bitterly cursed that woman, "Sister, you are such a noble person, you cannot be compared to her." Thus, her final decision was to leave. On the night she was preparing to leave, everything was arranged. Unexpectedly, a murder case occurred in her own home, and she was recognized by the secret agents of the Public Security Bureau and arrested together with the others. When Zheng Lie rushed over with a belly full of resentment, the deed was already done. In the end, Miss Yang left these last words: "For my son, for the love between mother and child, I have endured the pain of invisible shackles in this world, living a life of an invisible prison, and my spirit has never had a comfortable day. Now, I am free to endure the pain of visible shackles, living a life of a visible prison, and my spirit is finally much happier..." Zheng Lie only stood there dumbfounded, tearing the letter that Miss Yang had prepared for him into pieces. The tragedy ends here.

This play is divided into three acts. The first act depicts Zheng Lie's cousin, Jun Tao, learning that his wife, Jing Fang, has lost a lot of money at the Great China Club and scolding her, leading to a quarrel. This is where the author first plants the lead of the gambling den. The downfall of the insidious Zhou Jize is also written in this act. The author seems very clever. In the third act, he puts the story of Jun Tao and Jing Fang back in to echo each other. Moreover, the author does not inflict this shooting tragedy on Miss Yang, but adds it to Jing Fang as a kind of tense contrast. This structure is commendable. As for the author's highest point of expression, I believe it is at the end of the second act. In those dialogues, he fully includes the social issues we want to study.

Miss Yang: For example, when you were just in my arms, my hometown encountered a natural disaster, and my parents all died. We drifted to Shanghai. I didn't have the spirit to fight for life, and I couldn't bear the affection between siblings, so I abandoned you, or, as you said, I was determined to take you and jump into the sea. Then, would there still be a Chief of Public Security who graduated from the Department of Political Economy today? The Miss Yang had to raise her fatherless and homeless child and cultivate him to become a useful person. She had to think more about making some profit, so she took that easy path. Of course, she can be forgiven.

Zheng Lie: But I don't know how to forgive her. You can't compare her to your sister. My sister fought for me and society alone, without using dirty money like gambling dens to raise me and cultivate me.

Miss Yang: I did not use such dirty money to raise you and cultivate you. That is my happiness... Speaking of which... it's heartbreaking.

Zheng Lie: In order to protect their mother and child, the entire society is uneasy, and the entire society is sacrificed for them. This is the most painful thing in my hometown.

Miss Yang: You... must... call her... to take... her son... to jump... into the... sea... You... how can you... have... such... a cruel heart?

Zheng Lie: But you must also compare the advantages and disadvantages, and you cannot let major sacrifices be in the hands of small sacrifices.

Miss Yang: But the current world does not make everyone's life stable, which is different from forcing people not to have a stable life. If that Miss Yang could find a decent job for women, she would never be willing to take that path. Society forces her. If society has major sacrifices, society should bear this responsibility. In short, she is a good person. She is a pitiful woman, so she ended up in this tragic situation.

Zheng Lie: I don't want to have this view... In any case, in order to maintain a simple family for their mother and child, it is wrong to let the happiness of many families be ruined for her... It is only when people sacrifice themselves to save the entire society that many families can have true and great personalities. It is always unreasonable to let society become unstable for one person and to sacrifice the few for the many. Moreover, it is not necessarily reliable to cultivate a talent with the money sacrificed by many people.

If we look at these with a social perspective and analyze them fairly, will our conclusion about Miss Yang's background and actions be contempt or respect?

When he advises Jing Fang, how sincere are his words?

Fu Wuzhi: Please don't say that. Your husband also loves you deeply, so he is a little serious about exaggerating. He is full of good intentions. You can forgive him.

Your husband, Mr. Fu, thinks highly of you and doesn't want you to have any mistakes. His concern for you is exactly where his sincerity is expressed. He values you and respects you, so he treats your five-point fault as a ten-point fault. You don't have to be angry.

Perhaps this is a warning to general new women?

In terms of thought, the author may not have been thorough, and in some places in these three acts, I cannot find the author's consistent highest point of thought. This is a pity for the author.

As for the artistic stage, some places show that the author's narrative technique is uneconomical, and some sentences can be reduced, while some sentences should be strengthened to make the plot more powerful. Secondly, the loose structure is also unavoidable, but this is just my personal opinion. Fortunately, it is not a play review. My main purpose is to introduce and also to dedicate to the author's intention.

In short, in the current impoverished drama scene, this three-act play "Miss Yang's Secret" is more worthy of reading. The author's attitude towards drama is faithful. I hope that the author will continue to work hard in the future to fulfill the pioneering responsibility for Chinese drama and express it from more powerful creations.

Submission Guidelines for Book and Newspaper Introductions 208

(1) The text is limited to introducing the latest publications, both domestic and foreign. In addition to literary works, all books and newspapers on politics, economics, law, science, education, etc. are included in the introduction, but boring books and newspapers will not be introduced.

(2) Pictures are limited to the latest works of new domestic and foreign writers and related illustrations to the text.

(3) Articles on textual research or research on ancient Chinese books will not be published.

(4) Pictures and photos are limited to those related to the text.

(5) Each article should not exceed three thousand characters.

(6) Do not write on both sides of the manuscript paper.

(7) Do not write the manuscript horizontally, and do not write in a sloppy manner. Western text can be omitted if possible. If it is necessary to quote the original text, it must be attached below the translated text.

(8) Do not draw pictures with blue ink and do not use colored paper. Use white paper and black drawings.

(9) Please indicate that the manuscript is collected by the Book and Newspaper Introduction Editorial Department.

(10) Whether the submitted manuscript is published or not, it will not be returned. If it needs to be returned, it should be stated in advance and sufficient postage stamps should be attached.
